<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genomic insights into salt adaptation in a desert poplar"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2013 4:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2013-11-21"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2013 Nature Publishing Group, a division of Macmillan Publishers Limited. All Rights Reserved."/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Despite the high economic and ecological importance of forests, our knowledge of the genomic evolution of trees under salt stress remains very limited. Here we report the genome sequence of the desert poplar, Populus euphratica, which exhibits high tolerance to salt stress. Its genome is very similar and collinear to that of the closely related mesophytic congener, P. trichocarpa. However, we find that several gene families likely to be involved in tolerance to salt stress contain significantly more gene copies within the P. euphratica lineage. Furthermore, genes showing evidence of positive selection are significantly enriched in functional categories related to salt stress. Some of these genes, and others within the same categories, are significantly upregulated under salt stress relative to their expression in another salt-sensitive poplar. Our results provide an important background for understanding tree adaptation to salt stress and facilitating the genetic improvement of cultivated poplars for saline soils. Little is known about the genes that confer salt tolerance in trees. Here, Ma et al. report the genome sequence of the desert poplar, Populus euphratica, and provide insight into the genetic architecture and adaptation of this salt tolerant desert poplar.
                  
                    
                  
                "/>

Tuskan</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff8">8</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Oak Ridge National Laboratory" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.135519.a, 0000 0004 0446 2659, BioSciences Division, Oak Ridge National Laboratory, Oak Ridge, 37831, Tennessee, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Jun-Wang" data-author-popup="auth-Jun-Wang" data-corresp-id="c1">Jun Wang<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a>,<a href="#Aff9">9</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="BGI-Shenzhen" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.21155.32, 0000 0001 2034 1839, BGI-Shenzhen, Shenzhen, 518083, China" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of Copenhagen" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.5254.6, 0000 0001 0674 042X, Department of Biology, University of Copenhagen, Copenhagen, 1017, Denmark" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Jianquan-Liu" data-author-popup="auth-Jianquan-Liu" data-corresp-id="c2">Jianquan Liu<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="State Key Laboratory of Grassland Agro-Ecosystem, School of Life Sciences, Lanzhou University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.32566.34, 0000 0000 8571 0482, State Key Laboratory of Grassland Agro-Ecosystem, School of Life Sciences, Lanzhou University, Lanzhou, 730000, China" /></span></sup>Â </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Despite the high economic and ecological importance of forests, our knowledge of the genomic evolution of trees under salt stress remains very limited. Here we report the genome sequence of the desert poplar, <i>Populus euphratica</i>, which exhibits high tolerance to salt stress. Its genome is very similar and collinear to that of the closely related mesophytic congener, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>. However, we find that several gene families likely to be involved in tolerance to salt stress contain significantly more gene copies within the <i>P. euphratica</i> lineage. Furthermore, genes showing evidence of positive selection are significantly enriched in functional categories related to salt stress. Some of these genes, and others within the same categories, are significantly upregulated under salt stress relative to their expression in another salt-sensitive poplar. Our results provide an important background for understanding tree adaptation to salt stress and facilitating the genetic improvement of cultivated poplars for saline soils.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Forests dominate much of the terrestrial landscape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Food and Agricultural Organization of the United Nations. State of the Worldâs Forests FAO (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d37838e989">1</a></sup>. However, forest trees rarely occur on saline soils and little is known of the genetic basis of their tolerance to salt stress despite strong demand for their cultivation on highly saline soils in many parts of the world<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Oh, D. H., Dassanayake, M., Bohnert, H. J. &amp; Cheeseman, J. M. Life at the extreme: lessons from the genome. Genome Biol. 13, 241 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d37838e993">2</a></sup>. Members of the genus <i>Populus</i> are used as a model forest species for diverse studies not only because of their amenability to experimental and genetic manipulation, but also because of their high economic and ecological importance as the most widely cultivated tree throughout the northern hemisphere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Jansson, S. &amp; Douglas, C. J. Populus: a model system for plant biology. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 58, 435â458 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1000">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Jansson, S., Bhalarao, R. P. &amp; Groover, A. T. Genetics and Genomics of Populus Springer (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1003">4</a></sup>. More than 30 wild <i>Populus</i> species occur across diverse habitats over a wide geographical range, thereby providing an excellent system for unravelling the genetic bases of adaptive divergence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Jansson, S., Bhalarao, R. P. &amp; Groover, A. T. Genetics and Genomics of Populus Springer (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1011">4</a></sup>. <i>Populus euphratica</i> Oliv., which is native to desert regions ranging from western China to North Africa, is characterized by extraordinary adaptation to salt stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Gries, D. et al. Growth and water relations of Tamarix ramosissima and Populus euphratica on Taklamakan desert dunes in relation to depth to a permanent water table. Plant Cell Environ. 26, 725â736 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1018">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Brinker, M. et al. Linking the salt transcriptome with physiological responses of a salt-resistant Populus species as a strategy to identify genes important for stress acclimation. Plant Physiol. 154, 1697â1709 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1021">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Brosche, M. et al. Gene expression and metabolite profiling of Populus euphratica growing in the Negev desert. Genome Biol. 6, R101 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1024">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Ottow, E. A. et al. Populus euphratica displays apoplastic sodium accumulation, osmotic adjustment by decreases in calcium and soluble carbohydrates, and develops leaf succulence under salt stress. Plant Physiol. 139, 1762â1772 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1027">8</a></sup>. Notably, at high salinity it maintains higher growth and photosynthetic rates than other poplar species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Janz, D. et al. Salt stress induces the formation of a novel type of âpressure woodâ in two Populus species. N. Phytol. 194, 129â141 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1031">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Wang, R. et al. Leaf photosynthesis, fluorescence response to salinity and the relevance to chloroplast salt compartmentation and anti-oxidative stress in two poplars. Trees 21, 581â591 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1034">10</a></sup> and can survive concentrations of NaCl in nutrient solution up to 450âmM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Gu, R. et al. Transcript identification and profiling during salt stress and recovery of Populus euphratica. Tree Physiol. 24, 265â276 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1038">11</a></sup>.</p><p>In this study, we examine genomic differences between a xeric desert poplar and its mesophytic congener, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, for which a high-quality reference genome is available<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1048">12</a></sup>. We further examine gene expression differences following salt stress treatment in a comparison with another salt-sensitive congener, <i>P. tomentosa</i>. Our comparisons highlight the genetic bases of salt tolerance in the desert poplar.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Because of the limitations of next-generation sequencing for complex genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Alkan, C., Sajjadian, S. &amp; Eichler, E. E. Limitations of next-generation genome sequence assembly. Nat. Meth. 8, 61â65 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1067">13</a></sup> and the high levels of polymorphism found in this non-domesticated and open-pollinated species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Fig. S1</a>), we employed a newly developed fosmid-pooling strategy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Zhang, G. et al. The oyster genome reveals stress adaptation and complexity of shell formation. Nature 490, 49â54 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1074">14</a></sup> to sequence and assemble the <i>P. euphratica</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Methods</a>). Hierarchical assembly using 67.1âGb (~112 Ã ) whole-genome shotgun reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S1</a>), combined with more than 200 Ã high-quality reads from 66,240 fosmid clones (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S2</a>), yielded a final assembly with a total length of 496.5âMb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S3</a>), representing 83.7% of the <i>P. euphratica</i> nucleotide space (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Tables S4 and S5</a>). The contig N50 of the assembled sequence was 40.4âKb (longest, 728.4âKb) and scaffold N50 was 482âKb (longest, 8.8âMb; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Tab1">Table 1</a>), which were comparable to those of other plant genome assemblies generated by next-generation sequencing technology (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S6</a>). Sequencing depth distribution showed that over 92.5% of the assembly was covered by more than 20 Ã (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Figs S2 and S3</a>), ensuring a high single-base accuracy. The heterozygosity level in <i>P. euphratica</i> was ~0.5% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Tables S7 and S8</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Fig. S4</a>), which is almost twice that in <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (0.26%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1126">12</a></sup>. The assembly covered 97.3% of the 516,712 <i>Populus</i> expressed sequence tags (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S9</a>) and 97.7% of the 7 complete fosmids sequenced by Sanger sequencing (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Fig. S5</a>), without any obvious misassembly occurring. The coverage of the core eukaryotic genes was estimated to be 94.35% for the <i>P. euphratica</i> assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S11</a>), which is comparable to the estimate for <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (93.95%). All of these statistics supported that our draft genome sequence has high contiguity, coverage and accuracy, further demonstrating the feasibility of this hierarchical approach for <i>de novo</i> sequencing and assembly of a complex genome with high heterozygosity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Zhang, G. et al. The oyster genome reveals stress adaptation and complexity of shell formation. Nature 490, 49â54 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1155">14</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics for the assembly of the <i>Populus euphratica</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms3797/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome annotation</h3><p>Using a combination of homology-based searches and <i>de novo</i> annotation, we found that ~44% of the <i>P. euphratica</i> genome is composed of repetitive elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S12</a>), similar to that of the <i>P. trichocarpa</i> genome (47%; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Long-terminal repeats (LTRs) were the most abundant repeat class, representing 36.7% and 33.1% of the <i>P. euphratica</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> genomes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S13</a>). The distribution of repeat divergence rates revealed a peak of gypsy LTR at 11% in <i>P. euphratica</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Fig. S6</a>), which is likely to reflect a relatively recent expansion of this LTR family in the <i>P. euphratica</i> lineage.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Collinearity between the P. euphratica and P. trichocarpa genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Collinearity between the <i>P. euphratica</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3797/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3797/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3797_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3797/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3797_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="363" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>The <i>P. euphratica</i> scaffolds (blue) inferred to be collinear are linked (grey lines) to <i>P. trichocarpa</i> chromosomes (orange). The proportion of the repeat elements (left) across the chromosomes is indicated for 400âkb sliding windows at 50âkb steps. DNA transposable elements are shown in purple, long interspersed elements (LINE) are shown in light blue, Copia and Gypsy elements of LTR retrotransposons are shown in yellow and green, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3797/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A total of 34,279 protein-coding genes were predicted to be present in the <i>P. euphratica</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S14</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Fig. S7</a>), 96.6% of which were supported by expressed sequence tags and/or homology-based searching with only 3.4% derived solely from <i>de novo</i> gene predictions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Fig. S8</a>). Functional annotation confirmed that 94.3% of the predicted genes had known homologues in protein databases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S15</a>). Small RNA sequencing data supported the occurrence of 152 conserved and 114 candidate novel microRNAs predicted from the <i>P. euphratica</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S16</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Data 1 and 2</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Figs S9âS11</a>), most of which were extensively up/downregulated in response to salt stress (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S17</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Fig. S12</a>). In addition, we also identified 764 transfer RNAs, 706 ribosomal RNAs and 4,826 small nuclear RNAs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S18</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome evolution</h3><p>In accordance with previous research<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1472">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97â100 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1475">15</a></sup>, the distribution of the fourfold degenerate synonymous sites of the third codons (4DTv) value between duplicated genes showed similar peaks (~0.09 and ~0.59) in both <i>P. euphratica</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> genomes, suggesting that two ancient whole-genome duplication (WGD) events had occurred in the <i>Populus</i> lineage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S19</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Figs S13 and S14</a>). These shared WGDs were also confirmed by the extensive collinearity between the genomes of both species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). A total of 1,214 collinear blocks &gt;10âkb in length, corresponding to 323âMb (65% of the assembly) and 332âMb (76%) in the <i>P. euphratica</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> genomes, respectively, were identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S20</a>). Assuming that the recent WGD occurred around 65 million years ago (Mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1507">12</a></sup>, divergence between <i>P. euphratica</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> can be placed to ~14 Mya (4DTv, ~0.02), which approximates to that estimated from phylogenetic analysis (~8 Mya; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Fig. S15</a>).</p><p>We identified and designed a total of 18,938 universal pairs of simple sequence repeat primers in the collinear regions, which can be converted into genetic markers across most poplar species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Data 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S21</a>). These simple sequence repeat markers, as well as the intraspecific or interspecific nucleotide variation in collinear regions, will facilitate genetic dissection of agronomically important traits and accelerate the genetic improvement of cultivated poplars, particularly for growth on saline soils.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Adaptation to a saline environment</h3><p>Copy number within gene families has been reported to vary greatly between closely related, divergent species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Dassanayake, M. et al. The genome of the extremophile crucifer Thellungiella parvula. Nat. Genet. 43, 913â918 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1538">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Dassanayake, M., Oh, D. H., Hong, H., Bohnert, H. J. &amp; Cheeseman, J. M. Transcription strength and halophytic lifestyle. Trends Plant Sci. 16, 1â3 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1541">17</a></sup>. Both gene family and InterProScan domain analysis revealed that several gene families related to salt stress were substantially expanded in <i>P. euphratica</i> compared with other plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Tables S22âS24</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Figs S16 and S17</a>). For example, the <i>HKT1</i> (high-affinity K<sup>+</sup> transporter 1) gene family, which encodes Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> transporters that have important roles in affecting or determining salt tolerance in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Ren, Z. H. et al. A rice quantitative trait locus for salt tolerance encodes a sodium transporter. Nat. Genet. 37, 1141â1146 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1567">18</a></sup>, expanded from one member in the <i>P. trichocarpa</i> genome to four in the <i>P. euphratica</i> genome. Three of these genes occurred as tandem duplicates in <i>P. euphratica</i>, which together corresponded to a <i>HKT1-</i>like pseudogene in <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). <i>HKT1</i> transporters have a key role in limiting Na<sup>+</sup> transport from roots to shoots in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Davenport, R. J. et al. The Na+ transporter AtHKT1;1 controls retrieval of Na+ from the xylem in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell Environ. 30, 497â507 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1598">19</a></sup>, and may account for the lower rate of ion uptake and transport recorded in <i>P. euphratica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Janz, D. et al. Salt stress induces the formation of a novel type of âpressure woodâ in two Populus species. N. Phytol. 194, 129â141 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1604">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Chen, S., Li, J., Fritz, E., Wang, S. &amp; HÃ¼ttermann, A. Sodium and chloride distribution in roots and transport in three poplar genotypes under increasing NaCl stress. For. Ecol. Manage 168, 217â230 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1607">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Chen, S. et al. Effects of NaCl on shoot growth, transpiration, ion compartmentation, and transport in regenerated plants of Populus euphratica and Populus tomentosa. Can. J. For. Res. 33, 967â975 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1610">21</a></sup>. The gene family encoding P-type H<sup>+</sup>-ATPases also had more copies in the <i>P. euphratica</i> genome than in the <i>P. trichocarpa</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). These P-type ATPases provide the basic energy for Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> antiporters by sustaining an electrochemical H<sup>+</sup> gradient across the plasma membrane, thus making an important contribution to maintenance of low Na<sup>+</sup> concentrations in <i>P. euphratica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Chen, S. &amp; Polle, A. Salinity tolerance of Populus. Plant Biol. 12, 317â333 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1637">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Yang, Y. et al. A novel method to quantify H+-ATPase-dependent Na+ transport across plasma membrane vesicles. Biochim. Biophys. Acta 1768, 2078â2088 2007." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1640">23</a></sup>. Other expanded gene families include those encoding antioxidative enzymes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Noctor, G. &amp; Foyer, C. H. Ascorbate and glutathione: keeping active oxygen under control. Annu. Rev. Plant Physiol. Plant Mol. Biol. 49, 249â279 (1998)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1644">24</a></sup>, such as <i>CAT</i> (catalase) and <i>GR1</i> (glutathione reductase), and genes involved in abscisic acid (ABA) signalling regulation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Hirayama, T. &amp; Shinozaki, K. Perception and transduction of abscisic acid signals: keys to the function of the versatile plant hormone ABA. Trends Plant Sci. 12, 343â351 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1654">25</a></sup>, such as <i>GCR2</i> (G-protein-coupled receptor 2) and <i>PLD</i> (phospholipase D). Heat-shock proteins usually protect cells against salinity by controlling the proper folding and conformation of proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Wang, W., Vinocur, B., Shoseyov, O. &amp; Altman, A. Role of plant heat-shock proteins and molecular chaperones in the abiotic stress response. Trends Plant Sci. 9, 244â252 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1665">26</a></sup>. Several of these families (for example, <i>HSP20</i>, <i>HSP70</i> and <i>HSP90</i>) were expanded in the <i>P. euphratica</i> genome. In addition, the <i>P. euphratica</i> genome has more copies of <i>BADH</i> (betaine aldehyde dehydrogenase) and <i>GolS4</i> (galactinol synthase 4), which encode key enzymes involved in biosynthesis of critical solutes that have roles in osmotic adjustment pathways under salt stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Bartels, D. &amp; Sunkar, R. Drought and salt tolerance in plants. Crit. Rev. Plant Sci. 24, 23â58 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1691">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Taji, T. et al. Important roles of drought- and cold-inducible genes for galactinol synthase in stress tolerance in Arabidopsis thaliana. Plant J. 29, 417â426 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1694">28</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Adaptation of P. euphratica to salt stress."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Adaptation of <i>P. euphratica</i> to salt stress.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3797/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3797/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3797_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3797/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3797_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="575" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Comparison of the proportions of expanded genes in the <i>P. euphratica</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> lineages relative to their common ancestor. The number of expanded genes of each class is indicated above each bar. (<b>b</b>) Tandem duplications of <i>HKT1</i> genes. Note that the <i>PtrHKT1-</i>like gene is pseudogenized in <i>P. trichocarpa</i>. (<b>c</b>) PSGs and expanded key genes in salt-stress response pathways of <i>P. euphratica</i>. Boxes with borders indicate PSGs (red) and expanded (black) <i>P. euphratica</i> genes, and the filled colors correspond to their degree of regulation in FPKM<sub>treatment</sub>/FPKM<sub>control</sub> in response to salt stress. (<b>d</b>) Comparison of the proportions of PSGs in the <i>P. euphratica</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> lineages. The number of PSGs of each class is indicated above each bar. FPKM, fragments per kilobase of exon per million fragments mapped.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3797/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Adaptive divergence at the molecular level may also be reflected by an increased rate of non-synonymous changes within genes involved in adaptation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Qiu, Q. et al. The yak genome and adaptation to life at high altitude. Nat. Genet. 44, 946â949 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1770">29</a></sup>. In collinear regions, we identified 18,262 high-confidence 1:1 orthologous genes between <i>P. euphratica</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, with a mean protein similarity close to 98.94% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Fig. S18</a>). The genes with elevated pairwise genetic differentiation were primarily enriched in âphotosynthetic electron transport chainâ, âheat acclimationâ, âoxidoreductase activityâ and âcation channel activityâ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S25</a>), indicating rapid evolution and/or adaptive divergence in these functions between <i>P. euphratica</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i>. Of the 6,545 high-confidence orthologues identified among 10 plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Fig. S16</a>), we detected 57 positively selected genes (PSGs) in the <i>P. euphratica</i> lineage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S26</a>), which is significantly greater than the number (35 PSGs) in the <i>P. trichocarpa</i> lineage (<i>P</i>-value=0.014 by the Fisherâs exact test). Compared with <i>P. trichocarpa</i> PSGs, <i>P. euphratica</i> PSGs were significantly enriched (<i>P</i>-value â¤0.05 by the Fisherâs exact test) in âresponse to stimulusâ, âcation bindingâ and âoxidoreductase activityâ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a>). They included <i>ENH1</i> (enhancer of <i>sos3-1</i>), which encodes a chloroplast-localized rubredoxin-like protein and has an important role in mediation of both ion homeostasis and reactive oxygen species detoxification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Zhu, J. et al. An enhancer mutant of Arabidopsis salt overly sensitive 3 mediates both ion homeostasis and the oxidative stress response. Mol. Cell Biol. 27, 5214â5224 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1828">30</a></sup>; <i>CIPK1</i> (CBL-interacting protein kinase 1), a protein kinase interacting strongly with the calcium sensors CBL1 and CBL9, and alternatively controlling ABA-dependent and ABA-independent stress responses in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="D'Angelo, C. et al. Alternative complex formation of the Ca-regulated protein kinase CIPK1 controls abscisic acid-dependent and independent stress responses in Arabidopsis. Plant J. 48, 857â872 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1837">31</a></sup>; and <i>PSD1</i> (phosphatidylserine decarboxylase 1) encoding a crucial enzyme catalysing production of phosphatidylethanolamine and therefore raising stress tolerance by increasing the flexibility of cell membranes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Larsson, K. E., Nystrom, B. &amp; Liljenberg, C. A phosphatidylserine decarboxylase activity in root cells of oat (Avena sativa) is involved in altering membrane phospholipid composition during drought stress acclimation. Plant Physiol. 44, 211â219 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1845">32</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). Several genes encoding transcription factors such as HB40, bHLH87 and AP2/ERF, and oxidoreductases such as peroxidase, 2-oxoglutarate and Fe(II)-dependent oxygenase, also showed signs of positive selection.</p><p>To examine the genome-wide responses to salt stress of this desert poplar, we performed a series of deep transcriptome sequencings (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S27</a>) that identified 6,727, 3,954 and 3,733 genes that were differentially expressed in salt-stressed calluses, leaves and roots of seedlings, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Data 4â6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Fig. S19</a>). These differentially expressed genes (DEGs), which included those comprising expanded gene families and also those bearing the signature of positive selection (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S28</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Figs S20 and S21</a>), were similarly enriched in functional categories, such as âoxidoreductase activityâ, âtranscription factor activityâ and âion transportâ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). Several expanded gene families in the <i>P</i>. <i>euphratica</i> genome comprised transcription factors (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S24</a>), for example, <i>Myb</i>, <i>ERF</i>, <i>bZIP</i> and <i>WRKY</i>, having a role in the regulation of gene expression in response to abiotic stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Yamaguchi-Shinozaki, K. &amp; Shinozaki, K. Organization of cis-acting regulatory elements in osmotic- and cold-stress-responsive promoters. Trends Plant Sci. 10, 88â94 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1899">33</a></sup>. Some of these were extensively upregulated in response to salt stress (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Tables S28 and S29</a>). Furthermore, the key genes regulating Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> antiporters and controlling ion homeostasis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Zhu, J. K. Plant salt tolerance. Trends Plant Sci. 6, 66â71 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1914">34</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S30</a>), for example, <i>NHX1</i> (Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> exchanger 1), <i>SOS2</i> (salt overly sensitive 2) and <i>SOS3</i>, and those involved in the biosynthesis of ABA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Seo, M. &amp; Koshiba, T. Complex regulation of ABA biosynthesis in plants. Trends Plant Sci. 7, 41â48 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1935">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Du, H. et al. Characterization of the beta-carotene hydroxylase gene DSM2 conferring drought and oxidative stress resistance by increasing xanthophylls and abscisic acid synthesis in rice. Plant Physiol. 154, 1304â1318 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1938">36</a></sup>, for example, <i>BCH1</i> (Î²-carotene hydroxylase 1) and <i>ZEP</i> (zeaxanthin epoxidase), were upregulated in salt-stressed samples, which is consistent with previous research<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Qiu, Q. et al. Genome-scale transcriptome analysis of the desert poplar, Populus euphratica. Tree Physiol. 31, 452â461 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d37838e1949">37</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Comparative transcriptomics of P. euphratica and P. tomentosa under salt stress."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Comparative transcriptomics of <i>P. euphratica</i> and <i>P. tomentosa</i> under salt stress.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3797/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3797/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3797_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3797/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3797_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="708" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Functional category enrichment (<i>P</i>-value â¤0.05 by the Fisher's exact test) of DEGs in <i>P. euphratica</i> leaves (L), roots (R) and time-course profiles. (<b>b</b>) Venn diagram of the number of DEGs in <i>P. euphratica</i> and <i>P. tomentosa</i> under salt stress (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Methods</a>). (<b>c</b>) DEGs proportions in <i>P. euphratica</i> and <i>P. tomentosa</i>. The number of DEGs of each class is indicated above each bar. (<b>d</b>) Expression of the DEGs identified in <i>P. euphratica</i> and/or <i>P. tomentosa</i>. The heatmap was generated from hierarchical cluster analysis of genes. (<b>e</b>) Transcript levels of the genes showing different expression patterns between <i>P. euphratica</i> (blue) and <i>P. tomentosa</i> (orange). The transcript levels were determined by fragments per kilobase of exon per million fragments mapped (FPKM).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3797/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We further compared the expression profiles of the <i>P. euphratica</i> calluses in response to salt stress with those of the <i>P. tomentosa</i> (a salt-sensitive poplar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Chen, S. et al. Effects of NaCl on shoot growth, transpiration, ion compartmentation, and transport in regenerated plants of Populus euphratica and Populus tomentosa. Can. J. For. Res. 33, 967â975 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2039">21</a></sup>) calluses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Table S31</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Figs S22 and S23</a>). The results showed that many of the DEGs (2,278) were specific to <i>P. euphratica</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig3">Fig 3b</a>), and that more genes involved in âcation transporterâ, âoxidoreductase activityâ and âresponse to abiotic stimulusâ were induced in <i>P. euphratica</i> than in <i>P. tomentosa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). Clustering analysis suggested that many of the DEGs exhibited different regulatory patterns in response to salinity between these two species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a>). For example, the K<sup>+</sup> uptake transporter <i>KUP3</i> was extensively upregulated after 24âh of salt stress in <i>P. euphratica</i>, but was maintained at control levels in <i>P. tomentosa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig3">Fig. 3e</a>), indicating a critical role of this gene in controlling K<sup>+</sup> homeostasis in <i>P. euphratica</i>. Transcript levels of this gene are strongly induced by K<sup>+</sup> starvation in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Kim, E. J., Kwak, J. M., Uozumi, N. &amp; Schroeder, J. I. AtKUP1: an Arabidopsis gene encoding high-affinity potassium transport activity. Plant Cell 10, 51â62 (1998)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2093">38</a></sup>. Previous research suggested that <i>PeNhaD1</i>, encoding a NhaD-type Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> antiporter, has a role in mediating sodium tolerance in <i>P. euphratica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Ottow, E. A. et al. Molecular characterization of PeNhaD1: the first member of the NhaD Na+/H+ antiporter family of plant origin. Plant Mol. Biol. 58, 75â88 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2107">39</a></sup>. Consistent with this, we identified two gene members encoding NhaD-type antiporters, both of which maintained transcript levels in <i>P. euphratica</i>, but which significantly reduced transcript levels under salt stress after 12âh in <i>P. tomentosa</i> before regaining control levels after 24âh (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig3">Fig. 3e</a>). Another transporter encoding gene, <i>NCL</i> (Na<sup>+</sup>/Ca<sup>2+</sup> exchanger-like protein), involved in the maintenance of Ca<sup>2+</sup> homeostasis under salt stress in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Wang, P. et al. A Na+/Ca2+ exchanger-like protein (AtNCL) involved in salt stress in Arabidopsis. J. Biol. Chem. 287, 44062â44070 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2132">40</a></sup>, was strongly upregulated in <i>P. euphratica</i> relative to its expression in salt-sensitive <i>P. tomentosa</i>. In addition, <i>SOS5</i>, a gene encoding a putative cell surface adhesion protein for the maintenance of cell wall integrity and architecture under salt stress in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Shi, H., Kim, Y., Guo, Y., Stevenson, B. &amp; Zhu, J. K. The Arabidopsis SOS5 locus encodes a putative cell surface adhesion protein and is required for normal cell expansion. Plant Cell 15, 19â32 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2148">41</a></sup>, was downregulated in <i>P. tomentosa</i> after 12âh of salt stress, in contrast to maintenance of transcript levels recorded in <i>P. euphratica</i>. We further found that the gene <i>SDIR1</i> (salt-and drought-induced ring finger 1), whose overexpression improves drought tolerance in transgenic rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Gao, T. et al. OsSDIR1 overexpression greatly improves drought tolerance in transgenic rice. Plant Mol. Biol. 76, 145â156 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2162">42</a></sup>, was specifically upregulated after 6âh and maintained high transcript levels until 12âh after salt stress in <i>P. euphratica</i>. Finally, transcription factors, such as <i>ERF3</i> and <i>NAC042</i>, and the oxidoreductases <i>AOX1D</i> (alternative oxidase 1D) and <i>NDA2</i> (alternative NAD(P)H dehydrogenase 2), exhibited different expression patterns under salt stress in <i>P. euphratica</i> relative to those recorded in <i>P. tomentosa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Fig. S24</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>Abiotic stress factors, especially salinity and drought, restrict plant biomass production and pose an increasing threat to sustainable agriculture and forestry worldwide. Numerous studies have been conducted on the genetic and molecular mechanisms underlying salt tolerance in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Ren, Z. H. et al. A rice quantitative trait locus for salt tolerance encodes a sodium transporter. Nat. Genet. 37, 1141â1146 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2200">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Davenport, R. J. et al. The Na+ transporter AtHKT1;1 controls retrieval of Na+ from the xylem in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell Environ. 30, 497â507 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2203">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Zhu, J. et al. An enhancer mutant of Arabidopsis salt overly sensitive 3 mediates both ion homeostasis and the oxidative stress response. Mol. Cell Biol. 27, 5214â5224 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2206">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="D'Angelo, C. et al. Alternative complex formation of the Ca-regulated protein kinase CIPK1 controls abscisic acid-dependent and independent stress responses in Arabidopsis. Plant J. 48, 857â872 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2209">31</a></sup>, and have included genomic analyses of the extremophiles <i>Thellungiella parvula</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Dassanayake, M. et al. The genome of the extremophile crucifer Thellungiella parvula. Nat. Genet. 43, 913â918 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2215">16</a></sup> and <i>T. salsuginea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Wu, H. J. et al. Insights into salt tolerance from the genome of Thellungiella salsuginea. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 12219â12224 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2221">43</a></sup>. However, our current understanding of these aspects of salt tolerance remains limited, especially for woody plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Chen, S. &amp; Polle, A. Salinity tolerance of Populus. Plant Biol. 12, 317â333 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2225">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Bartels, D. &amp; Sunkar, R. Drought and salt tolerance in plants. Crit. Rev. Plant Sci. 24, 23â58 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2228">27</a></sup>. <i>Populus euphratica</i> is an excellent candidate for the analysis of salt tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Chen, S. &amp; Polle, A. Salinity tolerance of Populus. Plant Biol. 12, 317â333 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2236">22</a></sup>, as it displays apoplastic sodium accumulation and develops leaf succulence after prolonged salt exposure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Ottow, E. A. et al. Populus euphratica displays apoplastic sodium accumulation, osmotic adjustment by decreases in calcium and soluble carbohydrates, and develops leaf succulence under salt stress. Plant Physiol. 139, 1762â1772 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2240">8</a></sup>. Consequently, in the last decade it has become a model for elucidating both physiological and molecular mechanisms of salt tolerance in tree species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Gries, D. et al. Growth and water relations of Tamarix ramosissima and Populus euphratica on Taklamakan desert dunes in relation to depth to a permanent water table. Plant Cell Environ. 26, 725â736 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2244">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Brinker, M. et al. Linking the salt transcriptome with physiological responses of a salt-resistant Populus species as a strategy to identify genes important for stress acclimation. Plant Physiol. 154, 1697â1709 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2247">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Brosche, M. et al. Gene expression and metabolite profiling of Populus euphratica growing in the Negev desert. Genome Biol. 6, R101 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2250">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Ottow, E. A. et al. Populus euphratica displays apoplastic sodium accumulation, osmotic adjustment by decreases in calcium and soluble carbohydrates, and develops leaf succulence under salt stress. Plant Physiol. 139, 1762â1772 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2253">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Janz, D. et al. Salt stress induces the formation of a novel type of âpressure woodâ in two Populus species. N. Phytol. 194, 129â141 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2256">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Wang, R. et al. Leaf photosynthesis, fluorescence response to salinity and the relevance to chloroplast salt compartmentation and anti-oxidative stress in two poplars. Trees 21, 581â591 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2259">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Gu, R. et al. Transcript identification and profiling during salt stress and recovery of Populus euphratica. Tree Physiol. 24, 265â276 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2263">11</a></sup>. Using a newly developed fosmid-pooling strategy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Zhang, G. et al. The oyster genome reveals stress adaptation and complexity of shell formation. Nature 490, 49â54 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2267">14</a></sup>, we sequenced and assembled the complex genome of <i>P. euphratica</i> with high heterozygosity and compared it with the closely related salt-sensitive model plant, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>.</p><p>We found that <i>P. euphratica</i> diverged from <i>P. trichocarpa</i> within the last 8 to 14 million years (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Fig. S15</a>). Although both species shared at least two WGDs and exhibited extensive collinearity across the gene space (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Fig. S13</a>), species-specific genes involved in stress tolerance, such as âion transportâ, âATPase activityâ, âtranscript factor activityâ and âoxidoreductase activityâ, were selectively expanded and/or positively selected in the <i>P. euphratica</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#Fig2">Fig. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Tables S23âS26</a>). In this regard, the Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> transporter <i>HKT1</i> is of particular interest, because it is similarly expanded as tandem duplicated copies in both <i>T. parvula</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Dassanayake, M. et al. The genome of the extremophile crucifer Thellungiella parvula. Nat. Genet. 43, 913â918 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2316">16</a></sup> and <i>T. salsuginea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Wu, H. J. et al. Insights into salt tolerance from the genome of Thellungiella salsuginea. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 12219â12224 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2322">43</a></sup>. Further functional analysis of this gene family is needed to understand its critical role in salt tolerance in plants. In addition, other genes involved in ion transport and homeostasis, such as <i>NhaD1</i>, <i>KUP3</i> and <i>NCL</i>, were distinctly upregulated under salt stress when compared with another salt-sensitive poplar, <i>P. tomentosa</i>. Our analyses taken together suggest that <i>P. euphratica</i> may have increased its salt tolerance through duplication and/or upregulation of multiple genes involved in ion transport and homeostasis. These findings are important for an improved understanding of tree adaptation to salt stress and for accelerating the genetic improvement of cultivated poplars for growth on saline soils.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Genomic DNA was extracted from callus induced from <i>P. euphratica</i> shoots. Paired-end and mate-pair Illumina libraries were constructed with multiple insert sizes (138â40âkb) according to the manufacturerâs instructions. In addition, 66,240 fosmid clones with 40âkb in length were randomly selected and two small insert (250 and 500âbp) libraries were constructed for each clone. All libraries were sequenced on the Illumina Genome Analyzer and HiSeq 2000 sequencing system. The raw reads were processed by removing low-quality reads, adapter sequences and possible contaminated reads. Then a hierarchical strategy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Zhang, G. et al. The oyster genome reveals stress adaptation and complexity of shell formation. Nature 490, 49â54 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2357">14</a></sup> was used for genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3797#MOESM760">Supplementary Methods</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Gene prediction</h3><p>We used the homology-based and <i>de novo</i> methods, as well as RNA-seq data, to predict genes in the <i>P. euphratica</i> genome. For homology-based gene prediction, protein sequences from five plants (<i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>Ricinus communis</i>, <i>Prunus persica</i>, <i>Cucumis sativus</i> and <i>Glycine max</i>) were initially mapped onto the <i>P. euphratica</i> genome using TBLASTN and the homologous genome sequences were aligned against the matching proteins using GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988â995 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2397">44</a></sup> for accurate spliced alignments. Next, we used the <i>de novo</i> gene prediction methods Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435âW439 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2404">45</a></sup> and GenScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Salamov, A. A. &amp; Solovyev, V. V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516â522 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2409">46</a></sup> to predict protein-coding genes, using parameters trained for <i>P. euphratica</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>. We then integrated the homologues and those from <i>de novo</i> approaches using GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Elsik, C. G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2422">47</a></sup> to produce a consensus gene set. In addition, we aligned all the RNA-seq reads to the reference genome by TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105â1111 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2426">48</a></sup>, assembled the transcripts using Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511â515 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2431">49</a></sup> and predicted the open reading frames from the resultant data. Finally, we combined the GLEAN set with the gene models produced from RNA-seq to generate a more confident gene set.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Collinear block and genome duplication identification</h3><p>Pairwise whole-genome alignment between <i>P. euphratica</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> was constructed using the BLAST algorithm, and the scaffolds of <i>P. euphratica</i> were anchored to the <i>P. trichocarpa</i> corresponding chromosomes based on the consensus order of matched regions. To detect the signature of genome duplication, the programme MCSCAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Tang, H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944â1954 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2455">50</a></sup> was used to define a duplicated block. At least five genes are required to call synteny. For each duplicated block, the 4DTv values were calculated and distributions were plotted.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Gene family clusters</h3><p>The protein-coding genes from nine plant species (<i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>Ricinus communis</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Thellungiella parvula</i>, <i>Carica papaya</i>, <i>Fragaria vesca</i>, <i>Prunus persica</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i> and <i>Oryza sativa</i>) were downloaded. The longest translation form was chosen to represent each gene, and stretches of genes encoding fewer than 50 amino acids were filtered out. The OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2495">51</a></sup> method was then used to cluster all the genes into paralogous and orthologous groups. The 1,776 single-copy gene families obtained from this analysis were used to reconstruct phylogenies and estimate divergence time using MrBayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Huelsenbeck, J. P. &amp; Ronquist, F. MRBAYES: Bayesian inference of phylogenetic trees. Bioinformatics 17, 754â755 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2499">52</a></sup> and the MCMCtree programme implemented in the Phylogenetic Analysis by Maximum Likelihood<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2504">53</a></sup>. Calibration times were obtained from the TimeTree database ( <a href="http://www.timetree.org/">http://www.timetree.org/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Identification of PSGs</h3><p>Using the orthologues identified by OrthoMCL as a raw data set, we first masked sites with low quality (<i>phred</i>-like quality score&lt;20) and that were detected as single nucleotide variants in <i>P. euphratica</i> coding sequences. We then aligned them using the codon option in the Probabilistic Alignment Kit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Loytynoja, A. &amp; Goldman, N. An algorithm for progressive multiple alignment of sequences with insertions. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 10557â10562 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3797#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d37838e2530">54</a></sup> programme for the detection of positive selection. Alignments shorter than 150âbp after removing sites with ambiguous data were discarded. Finally, we obtained 6,545 high-confidence orthologues within the two poplar species and at least three of the other eight species (<i>R. communis</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>T. parvula</i>, <i>C. papaya</i>, <i>F. vesca</i>, <i>P. persica</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>), averaging ~7.7 species per gene.
